			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Obesity</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">

<p><b>Obesity</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Medical_condition" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical condition">medical condition</a> in which excess <a href="/wiki/Body_fat" class="mw-redirect" title="Body fat">body fat</a> has accumulated to the extent that it may have a negative effect on health.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2015_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015-1">[1]</a></sup> People are generally considered obese when their <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">body mass index</a> (BMI), a measurement obtained by dividing a person's weight by the square of the person's height, is over <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7002294199500000000♠</span>30&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kg</a>/<a href="/wiki/Square_metre" title="Square metre">m<sup>2</sup></a></span>, with the range <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7002245166250000000♠</span>25–30&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kg</a>/<a href="/wiki/Square_metre" title="Square metre">m<sup>2</sup></a></span> defined as <a href="/wiki/Overweight" title="Overweight">overweight</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2015_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015-1">[1]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asian</a> countries use lower values.<sup id="cite_ref-Kan2005_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kan2005-2">[2]</a></sup> Obesity increases the likelihood of <a href="/wiki/Obesity-associated_morbidity" title="Obesity-associated morbidity">various diseases</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_diseases" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardiovascular diseases">heart disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus_type_2" title="Diabetes mellitus type 2">type 2 diabetes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obstructive_sleep_apnea" title="Obstructive sleep apnea">obstructive sleep apnea</a>, certain types of <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Osteoarthritis" title="Osteoarthritis">osteoarthritis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Obesity is most commonly caused by a combination of excessive <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food</a> intake, lack of physical activity, and <a href="/wiki/Polygenic_inheritance" class="mw-redirect" title="Polygenic inheritance">genetic susceptibility</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2015_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> A few cases are caused primarily by <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_disorder" class="mw-redirect" title="Endocrine disorder">endocrine disorders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medication" class="mw-redirect" title="Medication">medications</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Mental_illness" class="mw-redirect" title="Mental illness">mental illness</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ble2008_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ble2008-5">[5]</a></sup> The view that obese people eat little yet gain weight due to a slow metabolism is not generally supported.<sup id="cite_ref-OUP2011_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OUP2011-6">[6]</a></sup> On average, obese people have a greater energy expenditure than their thin counterparts due to the energy required to maintain an increased body mass.<sup id="cite_ref-OUP2011_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OUP2011-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Obesity is mostly preventable through a combination of social changes and personal choices.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2015_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015-1">[1]</a></sup> Changes to <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">diet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physical_exercise" title="Physical exercise">exercising</a> are the main treatments.<sup id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-3">[3]</a></sup> Diet quality can be improved by reducing the consumption of energy-dense foods, such as those high in fat and sugars, and by increasing the intake of <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2015_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anti-obesity_drug" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-obesity drug">Medications</a> may be taken, along with a suitable diet, to reduce appetite or decrease fat absorption.<sup id="cite_ref-Yan2014_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yan2014-8">[8]</a></sup> If diet, exercise, and medication are not effective, a <a href="/wiki/Gastric_balloon" title="Gastric balloon">gastric balloon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bariatric_surgery" title="Bariatric surgery">surgery</a> may be performed to reduce stomach volume or bowel length, leading to feeling full earlier or a reduced ability to absorb nutrients from food.<sup id="cite_ref-Col2014_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Col2014-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-balloon2008_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-balloon2008-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Obesity is a leading <a href="/wiki/Preventable_causes_of_death" title="Preventable causes of death">preventable cause of death</a> worldwide, with increasing rates in adults and <a href="/wiki/Childhood_obesity" title="Childhood obesity">children</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2015_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> In 2014, 600 million adults (13%) and 42 million children under the age of five were obese.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2015_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015-1">[1]</a></sup> Obesity is more common in women than men.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2015_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015-1">[1]</a></sup> Authorities view it as one of the most serious <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> problems of the 21st&#160;century.<sup id="cite_ref-Dibaise2013_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dibaise2013-12">[12]</a></sup> Obesity is <a href="/wiki/Stigmatized" class="mw-redirect" title="Stigmatized">stigmatized</a> in much of the modern world (particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a>), though it was seen as a symbol of wealth and <a href="/wiki/Fertility" title="Fertility">fertility</a> at other times in history and still is in some parts of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Woodhouse_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhouse-13">[13]</a></sup> In 2013, the <a href="/wiki/American_Medical_Association" title="American Medical Association">American Medical Association</a> classified obesity as a disease.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes20130618_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes20130618-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classification_of_obesity" title="Classification of obesity">Classification of obesity</a></div>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Obesity_%26_BMI.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Obesity_%26_BMI.png/260px-Obesity_%26_BMI.png" width="260" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Obesity_%26_BMI.png/390px-Obesity_%26_BMI.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Obesity_%26_BMI.png/520px-Obesity_%26_BMI.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1200" /></a>

Obesity and BMI</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Obesity6.JPG" class="image"><img alt="A front and side view of a &quot;super obese&quot; male torso. Stretch marks of the skin are visible along with gynecomastia." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Obesity6.JPG/260px-Obesity6.JPG" width="260" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Obesity6.JPG/390px-Obesity6.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Obesity6.JPG/520px-Obesity6.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3428" data-file-height="1641" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Obesity6.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A "super obese" male with a BMI of 53&#160;kg/m<sup>2</sup>: weight 182&#160;kg (400&#160;lb), height 185&#160;cm (6&#160;ft 1&#160;in)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Obesity is a <a href="/wiki/Medical_condition" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical condition">medical condition</a> in which excess <a href="/wiki/Body_fat" class="mw-redirect" title="Body fat">body fat</a> has accumulated to the extent that it may have an adverse effect on health.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO_2000_p.6_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_2000_p.6-17">[17]</a></sup> It is defined by <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">body mass index (BMI)</a> and further evaluated in terms of fat distribution via the <a href="/wiki/Waist-hip_ratio" class="mw-redirect" title="Waist-hip ratio">waist–hip ratio</a> and total cardiovascular risk factors.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweet2007_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweet2007-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> BMI is closely related to both <a href="/wiki/Body_fat_percentage" title="Body fat percentage">percentage body fat</a> and total body fat.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In children, a healthy weight varies with age and sex. Obesity in children and adolescents is defined not as an absolute number but in relation to a historical normal group, such that obesity is a BMI greater than the 95th&#160;<a href="/wiki/Percentile" title="Percentile">percentile</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cdc.gov_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cdc.gov-21">[21]</a></sup> The reference data on which these percentiles were based date from 1963 to 1994, and thus have not been affected by the recent increases in weight.<sup id="cite_ref-Flegal_KM.2C_Ogden_CL.2C_Wei_R.2C_Kuczmarski_RL.2C_Johnson_CL_2001_1086.E2.80.9393_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flegal_KM.2C_Ogden_CL.2C_Wei_R.2C_Kuczmarski_RL.2C_Johnson_CL_2001_1086.E2.80.9393-22">[22]</a></sup> BMI is defined as the subject's weight divided by the square of their height and is calculated as follows.</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi>
          <mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi>
          <mi mathvariant="normal">I</mi>
        </mrow>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>
            <mi>m</mi>
            <msup>
              <mi>h</mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>2</mn>
              </mrow>
            </msup>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathrm {BMI} ={\frac {m}{h^{2}}}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9da82c7bb2d89625a5dcff65a2e1af836a5773c7" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:11.004ex; height:5.009ex;" alt="{\mathrm  {BMI}}={\frac  {m}{h^{2}}}" /></span>,</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>where <i>m</i> and <i>h</i> are the subject's weight and height respectively.</dd>
</dl>
<p>BMI is usually expressed in kilograms per square metre, resulting when weight is measured in kilograms and height in metres. To convert from pounds per square inch multiply by <span class="nowrap">703 (kg/m<sup>2</sup>)/(lb/sq in)</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most commonly used definitions, established by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) in 1997 and published in 2000, provide the values listed in the table.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO_2000_p.9_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_2000_p.9-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some modifications to the WHO definitions have been made by particular organizations.<sup id="cite_ref-Bei2002_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bei2002-26">[26]</a></sup> The surgical literature breaks down class II and III obesity into further categories whose exact values are still disputed.<sup id="cite_ref-morbid2007_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morbid2007-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Any BMI ≥ 35 or 40&#160;kg/m<sup>2</sup> is <i>severe obesity</i>.</li>
<li>A BMI of ≥ 35&#160;kg/m<sup>2</sup> and experiencing obesity-related health conditions or ≥40–44.9&#160;kg/m<sup>2</sup> is <i>morbid obesity</i>.</li>
<li>A BMI of ≥ 45 or 50&#160;kg/m<sup>2</sup> is <i>super obesity</i>.</li>
</ul>
<p>As Asian populations develop negative health consequences at a lower BMI than Caucasians, some nations have redefined obesity; Japan have defined obesity as any BMI greater than 25&#160;kg/m<sup>2</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kan2005_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kan2005-2">[2]</a></sup> while China uses a BMI of greater than 28&#160;kg/m<sup>2</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bei2002_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bei2002-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_health"><span id="Effects_on_health"></span>Effects on health</span></h2>
<p>Excessive body <a href="/wiki/Human_weight" class="mw-redirect" title="Human weight">weight</a> is associated with various <a href="/wiki/Diseases" class="mw-redirect" title="Diseases">diseases</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_diseases" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardiovascular diseases">cardiovascular diseases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus_type_2" title="Diabetes mellitus type 2">diabetes mellitus type 2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obstructive_sleep_apnea" title="Obstructive sleep apnea">obstructive sleep apnea</a>, certain types of <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osteoarthritis" title="Osteoarthritis">osteoarthritis</a><sup id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-3">[3]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Poulain_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poulain-28">[28]</a></sup> As a result, obesity has been found to reduce <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mortality">Mortality</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:MenBMIMort.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/MenBMIMort.png/200px-MenBMIMort.png" width="200" height="157" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/MenBMIMort.png/300px-MenBMIMort.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/MenBMIMort.png/400px-MenBMIMort.png 2x" data-file-width="620" data-file-height="487" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:WomenBMIMort.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/WomenBMIMort.png/200px-WomenBMIMort.png" width="200" height="154" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/WomenBMIMort.png/300px-WomenBMIMort.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/WomenBMIMort.png/400px-WomenBMIMort.png 2x" data-file-width="641" data-file-height="493" /></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Relative risk of death over 10 years for white men (left) and women (right) who have never smoked in the United States by BMI.<sup id="cite_ref-NEJM10_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NEJM10-29">[29]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Obesity is one of the leading <a href="/wiki/Preventable_causes_of_death" title="Preventable causes of death">preventable causes of death</a> worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-Barn1999_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barn1999-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Allison_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allison-32">[32]</a></sup> A number of reviews have found that mortality risk is lowest at a BMI of 20–25&#160;kg/m<sup>2</sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lancet2009_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2009-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lancet2016Mort_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2016Mort-35">[35]</a></sup> in non-smokers and at 24–27&#160;kg/m<sup>2</sup> in current smokers, with risk increasing along with changes in either direction.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Euro2008_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Euro2008-37">[37]</a></sup> This appears to apply in at least four continents.<sup id="cite_ref-Lancet2016Mort_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2016Mort-35">[35]</a></sup> In contrast, a 2013 review found that grade 1 obesity (BMI 30-35) was not associated with higher mortality than normal weight, and that overweight (BMI 25-30) was associated with "lower" mortality than was normal weight (BMI 18.5-25).<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Other evidence suggests that the association of BMI and waist circumference with mortality is U- or J-shaped, while the association between waist-to-hip ratio and <a href="/wiki/Waist-to-height_ratio" title="Waist-to-height ratio">waist-to-height ratio</a> with mortality is more positive.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In Asians the risk of negative health effects begins to increase between 22–25&#160;kg/m<sup>2</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> A BMI above 32&#160;kg/m<sup>2</sup> has been associated with a doubled <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">mortality rate</a> among women over a 16-year period.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> In the United States obesity is estimated to cause 111,909 to 365,000 deaths per year,<sup id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Allison_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allison-32">[32]</a></sup> while 1 million (7.7%) of deaths in Europe are attributed to excess weight.<sup id="cite_ref-EuroG2008_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EuroG2008-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Euro2007_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Euro2007-43">[43]</a></sup> On average, obesity reduces life expectancy by six to seven&#160;years,<sup id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_3-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> a BMI of 30–35&#160;kg/m<sup>2</sup> reduces life expectancy by two to four&#160;years,<sup id="cite_ref-Lancet2009_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2009-34">[34]</a></sup> while severe obesity (BMI&#160;&gt;&#160;40&#160;kg/m<sup>2</sup>) reduces life expectancy by ten&#160;years.<sup id="cite_ref-Lancet2009_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2009-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morbidity">Morbidity</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Obesity-associated_morbidity" title="Obesity-associated morbidity">Obesity-associated morbidity</a></div>
<p>Obesity increases the risk of many physical and mental conditions. These comorbidities are most commonly shown in <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_syndrome" title="Metabolic syndrome">metabolic syndrome</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_3-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-3">[3]</a></sup> a combination of medical disorders which includes: <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus_type_2" title="Diabetes mellitus type 2">diabetes mellitus type 2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">high blood pressure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypercholesterolemia" title="Hypercholesterolemia">high blood cholesterol</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hypertriglyceridemia" title="Hypertriglyceridemia">high triglyceride levels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Complications are either directly caused by obesity or indirectly related through mechanisms sharing a common cause such as a poor diet or a <a href="/wiki/Sedentary_lifestyle" title="Sedentary lifestyle">sedentary lifestyle</a>. The strength of the link between obesity and specific conditions varies. One of the strongest is the link with <a href="/wiki/Type_2_diabetes" class="mw-redirect" title="Type 2 diabetes">type 2 diabetes</a>. Excess body fat underlies 64% of cases of diabetes in men and 77% of cases in women.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Health consequences fall into two broad categories: those attributable to the effects of increased fat mass (such as <a href="/wiki/Osteoarthritis" title="Osteoarthritis">osteoarthritis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obstructive_sleep_apnea" title="Obstructive sleep apnea">obstructive sleep apnea</a>, social stigmatization) and those due to the increased number of <a href="/wiki/Fat_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Fat cells">fat cells</a> (<a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">diabetes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-alcoholic_fatty_liver_disease" title="Non-alcoholic fatty liver disease">non-alcoholic fatty liver disease</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_3-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bray2004_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bray2004-47">[47]</a></sup> Increases in body fat alter the body's response to insulin, potentially leading to <a href="/wiki/Insulin_resistance" title="Insulin resistance">insulin resistance</a>. Increased fat also creates a <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">proinflammatory state</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Thrombosis" title="Thrombosis">prothrombotic</a> state.<sup id="cite_ref-Bray2004_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bray2004-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>

<td><a href="/wiki/Gastroenterology" title="Gastroenterology">Gastroenterology</a></td>
<td>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Survival_paradox">Survival paradox</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Obesity_paradox" title="Obesity paradox">Obesity paradox</a></div>
<p>Although the negative health consequences of obesity in the general population are well supported by the available evidence, health outcomes in certain subgroups seem to be improved at an increased BMI, a phenomenon known as the obesity survival paradox.<sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt2007_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2007-71">[71]</a></sup> The paradox was first described in 1999 in overweight and obese people undergoing hemodialysis,<sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt2007_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2007-71">[71]</a></sup> and has subsequently been found in those with <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_vascular_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Peripheral vascular disease">peripheral artery disease</a> (PAD).<sup id="cite_ref-paradox2003_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paradox2003-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>In people with heart failure, those with a BMI between 30.0 and 34.9 had lower mortality than those with a normal weight. This has been attributed to the fact that people often lose weight as they become progressively more ill.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Similar findings have been made in other types of heart disease. People with class I obesity and heart disease do not have greater rates of further heart problems than people of normal weight who also have heart disease. In people with greater degrees of obesity, however, the risk of further cardiovascular events is increased.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Even after <a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_bypass_surgery" title="Coronary artery bypass surgery">cardiac bypass surgery</a>, no increase in mortality is seen in the overweight and obese.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> One study found that the improved survival could be explained by the more aggressive treatment obese people receive after a cardiac event.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Another found that if one takes into account <a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">chronic obstructive pulmonary disease</a> (COPD) in those with PAD, the benefit of obesity no longer exists.<sup id="cite_ref-paradox2003_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paradox2003-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span></h2>
<p>At an individual level, a combination of excessive <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a> intake and a lack of <a href="/wiki/Physical_activity" class="mw-redirect" title="Physical activity">physical activity</a> is thought to explain most cases of obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-CADG2006_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CADG2006-78">[78]</a></sup> A limited number of cases are due primarily to genetics, medical reasons, or psychiatric illness.<sup id="cite_ref-Ble2008_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ble2008-5">[5]</a></sup> In contrast, increasing rates of obesity at a societal level are felt to be due to an easily accessible and palatable diet,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> increased reliance on cars, and mechanized manufacturing.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-James2008_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James2008-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2006 review identified ten other possible contributors to the recent increase of obesity: (1) <a href="/wiki/Sleep_debt" title="Sleep debt">insufficient sleep</a>, (2) <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_disruptor" title="Endocrine disruptor">endocrine disruptors</a> (environmental <a href="/wiki/Pollutant" title="Pollutant">pollutants</a> that interfere with lipid metabolism), (3) decreased variability in ambient temperature, (4) decreased rates of <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">smoking</a>, because smoking suppresses appetite, (5) increased use of medications that can cause weight gain (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Atypical_antipsychotics" class="mw-redirect" title="Atypical antipsychotics">atypical antipsychotics</a>), (6) proportional increases in ethnic and age groups that tend to be heavier, (7) pregnancy at a later age (which may cause susceptibility to obesity in children), (8) <a href="/wiki/Epigenetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Epigenetic">epigenetic</a> risk factors passed on generationally, (9) <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> for higher BMI, and (10) <a href="/wiki/Assortative_mating" title="Assortative mating">assortative mating</a> leading to increased concentration of obesity risk factors (this would increase the number of obese people by increasing population variance in weight).<sup id="cite_ref-pmid16801930_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16801930-82">[82]</a></sup> While there is substantial evidence supporting the influence of these mechanisms on the increased prevalence of obesity, the evidence is still inconclusive, and the authors state that these are probably less influential than the ones discussed in the previous paragraph.</p>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:World_Per_Person_Energy_Consumption.png" class="image"><img alt="A graph showing a gradual increase in global food energy consumption per person per day between 1961 and 2002." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/World_Per_Person_Energy_Consumption.png/350px-World_Per_Person_Energy_Consumption.png" width="350" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/World_Per_Person_Energy_Consumption.png/525px-World_Per_Person_Energy_Consumption.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/World_Per_Person_Energy_Consumption.png/700px-World_Per_Person_Energy_Consumption.png 2x" data-file-width="817" data-file-height="467" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:World_Per_Person_Energy_Consumption.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Average per capita energy consumption of the world from 1961 to 2002<sup id="cite_ref-Earth09_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Earth09-83">[83]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A 2016 review supported excess food as the primary factor.<sup id="cite_ref-pharmaReview_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pharmaReview-84">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dietary_energy_supply" title="Dietary energy supply">Dietary energy supply</a> per capita varies markedly between different regions and countries. It has also changed significantly over time.<sup id="cite_ref-Earth09_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Earth09-83">[83]</a></sup> From the early 1970s to the late 1990s the average food energy available per person per day (the amount of food bought) increased in all parts of the world except Eastern Europe. The United States had the highest availability with 3,654 calories (15,290&#160;kJ) per person in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-Earth09_83-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Earth09-83">[83]</a></sup> This increased further in 2003 to 3,754 calories (15,710&#160;kJ).<sup id="cite_ref-Earth09_83-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Earth09-83">[83]</a></sup> During the late 1990s Europeans had 3,394 calories (14,200&#160;kJ) per person, in the developing areas of Asia there were 2,648 calories (11,080&#160;kJ) per person, and in sub-Saharan Africa people had 2,176 calories (9,100&#160;kJ) per person.<sup id="cite_ref-Earth09_83-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Earth09-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Total food energy consumption has been found to be related to obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>The widespread availability of <a href="/wiki/Nutrition#Advice_and_guidance" title="Nutrition">nutritional guidelines</a><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> has done little to address the problems of overeating and poor dietary choice.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> From 1971 to 2000, obesity rates in the United States increased from 14.5% to 30.9%.<sup id="cite_ref-Flegal2002_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flegal2002-89">[89]</a></sup> During the same period, an increase occurred in the average amount of food energy consumed. For women, the average increase was 335 calories (1,400&#160;kJ) per day (1,542 calories (6,450&#160;kJ) in 1971 and 1,877 calories (7,850&#160;kJ) in 2004), while for men the average increase was 168 calories (700&#160;kJ) per day (2,450 calories (10,300&#160;kJ) in 1971 and 2,618 calories (10,950&#160;kJ) in 2004). Most of this extra food energy came from an increase in carbohydrate consumption rather than fat consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The primary sources of these extra carbohydrates are sweetened beverages, which now account for almost 25 percent of daily food energy in young adults in America,<sup id="cite_ref-Caballero_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caballero-91">[91]</a></sup> and potato chips.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Consumption of sweetened drinks such as soft drinks, fruit drinks, iced tea, and energy and vitamin water drinks is believed to be contributing to the rising rates of obesity<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> and to an increased risk of metabolic syndrome and type 2 diabetes.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D_deficiency" title="Vitamin D deficiency">Vitamin D deficiency</a> is related to diseases associated with obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-WambergPedersen2015_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WambergPedersen2015-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>As societies become increasingly reliant on <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">energy-dense</a>, big-portions, and fast-food meals, the association between fast-food consumption and obesity becomes more concerning.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> In the United States consumption of fast-food meals tripled and food energy intake from these meals quadrupled between 1977 and 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Agricultural_policy" title="Agricultural policy">Agricultural policy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution_(agriculture)" class="mw-redirect" title="Green Revolution (agriculture)">techniques</a> in the United States and Europe have led to lower food prices. In the United States, subsidization of corn, soy, wheat, and rice through the <a href="/wiki/U.S._farm_bill" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. farm bill">U.S. farm bill</a> has made the main sources of processed food cheap compared to fruits and vegetables.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Calorie_count_laws" title="Calorie count laws">Calorie count laws</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nutrition_facts_label" title="Nutrition facts label">nutrition facts labels</a> attempt to steer people toward making healthier food choices, including awareness of how much food energy is being consumed.</p>
<p>Obese people consistently under-report their food consumption as compared to people of normal weight.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> This is supported both by tests of people carried out in a <a href="/wiki/Calorimeter" title="Calorimeter">calorimeter</a> room<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> and by direct observation.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sedentary_lifestyle">Sedentary lifestyle</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sedentary_lifestyle" title="Sedentary lifestyle">Sedentary lifestyle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Exercise_trends" title="Exercise trends">Exercise trends</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Sedentary_lifestyle" title="Sedentary lifestyle">sedentary lifestyle</a> plays a significant role in obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Worldwide there has been a large shift towards less physically demanding work,<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2009_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2009-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WHOExercise_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOExercise-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ness2006_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ness2006-105">[105]</a></sup> and currently at least 30% of the world's population gets insufficient exercise.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOExercise_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOExercise-104">[104]</a></sup> This is primarily due to increasing use of mechanized transportation and a greater prevalence of labor-saving technology in the home.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2009_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2009-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WHOExercise_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOExercise-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ness2006_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ness2006-105">[105]</a></sup> In children, there appear to be declines in levels of physical activity due to less walking and physical education.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> World trends in active leisure time <a href="/wiki/Physical_activity" class="mw-redirect" title="Physical activity">physical activity</a> are less clear. The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> indicates people worldwide are taking up less active recreational pursuits, while a study from Finland<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> found an increase and a study from the United States found leisure-time physical activity has not changed significantly.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> A 2011 review of physical activity in children found that it may not be a significant contributor.<sup id="cite_ref-Wil2011_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wil2011-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>In both children and adults, there is an association between television viewing time and the risk of obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> A review found 63 of 73 studies (86%) showed an increased rate of childhood obesity with increased media exposure, with rates increasing proportionally to time spent watching television.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Genetics_of_obesity" title="Genetics of obesity">Genetics of obesity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:La_monstrua_desnuda_(1680),_de_Juan_Carre%C3%B1o_de_Miranda..jpg" class="image"><img alt="A painting of a dark haired pink cheeked obese nude young female leaning against a table. She is holding grapes and grape leaves in her left hand which cover her genitalia." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/La_monstrua_desnuda_%281680%29%2C_de_Juan_Carre%C3%B1o_de_Miranda..jpg/290px-La_monstrua_desnuda_%281680%29%2C_de_Juan_Carre%C3%B1o_de_Miranda..jpg" width="290" height="432" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/La_monstrua_desnuda_%281680%29%2C_de_Juan_Carre%C3%B1o_de_Miranda..jpg/435px-La_monstrua_desnuda_%281680%29%2C_de_Juan_Carre%C3%B1o_de_Miranda..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/La_monstrua_desnuda_%281680%29%2C_de_Juan_Carre%C3%B1o_de_Miranda..jpg/580px-La_monstrua_desnuda_%281680%29%2C_de_Juan_Carre%C3%B1o_de_Miranda..jpg 2x" data-file-width="2049" data-file-height="3051" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:La_monstrua_desnuda_(1680),_de_Juan_Carre%C3%B1o_de_Miranda..jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 1680 painting by <a href="/wiki/Juan_Carreno_de_Miranda" class="mw-redirect" title="Juan Carreno de Miranda">Juan Carreno de Miranda</a> of a girl presumed to have <a href="/wiki/Prader%E2%80%93Willi_syndrome" title="Prader–Willi syndrome">Prader–Willi syndrome</a><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Like many other medical conditions, obesity is the result of an interplay between genetic and environmental factors. <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology)" title="Polymorphism (biology)">Polymorphisms</a> in various <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> controlling <a href="/wiki/Appetite" title="Appetite">appetite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> predispose to obesity when sufficient food energy is present. As of 2006, more than 41 of these sites on the human genome have been linked to the development of obesity when a favorable environment is present.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> People with two copies of the <a href="/wiki/FTO_gene" title="FTO gene">FTO gene</a> (fat mass and obesity associated gene) have been found on average to weigh 3–4&#160;kg more and have a 1.67-fold greater risk of obesity compared with those without the risk <a href="/wiki/Allele" title="Allele">allele</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The differences in BMI between people that are <a href="/wiki/Heritability" title="Heritability">due to genetics</a> varies depending on the population examined from 6% to 85%.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>Obesity is a major feature in several syndromes, such as <a href="/wiki/Prader%E2%80%93Willi_syndrome" title="Prader–Willi syndrome">Prader–Willi syndrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bardet%E2%80%93Biedl_syndrome" title="Bardet–Biedl syndrome">Bardet–Biedl syndrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cohen_syndrome" title="Cohen syndrome">Cohen syndrome</a>, and <a href="/wiki/MOMO_syndrome" title="MOMO syndrome">MOMO syndrome</a>. (The term "non-syndromic obesity" is sometimes used to exclude these conditions.)<sup id="cite_ref-pmid19506576_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19506576-118">[118]</a></sup> In people with early-onset severe obesity (defined by an onset before 10&#160;years of age and body mass index over three <a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">standard deviations</a> above normal), 7% harbor a single point DNA mutation.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>Studies that have focused on inheritance patterns rather than on specific genes have found that 80% of the offspring of two <a href="/wiki/Parental_obesity" title="Parental obesity">obese parents</a> were also obese, in contrast to less than 10% of the offspring of two parents who were of normal weight.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Different people exposed to the same environment have different risks of obesity due to their underlying genetics.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Thrifty_gene_hypothesis" title="Thrifty gene hypothesis">thrifty gene hypothesis</a> postulates that, due to dietary scarcity during human evolution, people are prone to obesity. Their ability to take advantage of rare periods of abundance by storing energy as fat would be advantageous during times of varying food availability, and individuals with greater adipose reserves would be more likely to survive <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>. This tendency to store fat, however, would be maladaptive in societies with stable food supplies.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> This theory has received various criticisms, and other evolutionarily-based theories such as the <a href="/wiki/Drifty_gene_hypothesis" title="Drifty gene hypothesis">drifty gene hypothesis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Thrifty_phenotype" title="Thrifty phenotype">thrifty phenotype hypothesis</a> have also been proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_illnesses">Other illnesses</span></h3>
<p>Certain physical and mental illnesses and the pharmaceutical substances used to treat them can increase risk of obesity. Medical illnesses that increase obesity risk include several rare genetic syndromes (listed above) as well as some congenital or acquired conditions: <a href="/wiki/Hypothyroidism" title="Hypothyroidism">hypothyroidism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cushing%27s_syndrome" title="Cushing's syndrome">Cushing's syndrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Growth_hormone_deficiency" title="Growth hormone deficiency">growth hormone deficiency</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Eating_disorder" title="Eating disorder">eating disorders</a>: <a href="/wiki/Binge_eating_disorder" title="Binge eating disorder">binge eating disorder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Night_eating_syndrome" title="Night eating syndrome">night eating syndrome</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_3-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-3">[3]</a></sup> However, obesity is not regarded as a psychiatric disorder, and therefore is not listed in the <a href="/wiki/Diagnostic_and_Statistical_Manual_of_Mental_Disorders" title="Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders">DSM-IVR</a> as a psychiatric illness.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The risk of overweight and obesity is higher in patients with psychiatric disorders than in persons without psychiatric disorders.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>Certain medications may cause weight gain or changes in <a href="/wiki/Body_composition" title="Body composition">body composition</a>; these include <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfonylurea" title="Sulfonylurea">sulfonylureas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thiazolidinedione" title="Thiazolidinedione">thiazolidinediones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atypical_antipsychotic" title="Atypical antipsychotic">atypical antipsychotics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antidepressant" title="Antidepressant">antidepressants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glucocorticoids" class="mw-redirect" title="Glucocorticoids">steroids</a>, certain <a href="/wiki/Anticonvulsant" title="Anticonvulsant">anticonvulsants</a> (<a href="/wiki/Phenytoin" title="Phenytoin">phenytoin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Valproate" title="Valproate">valproate</a>), <a href="/wiki/Pizotifen" title="Pizotifen">pizotifen</a>, and some forms of <a href="/wiki/Hormonal_contraception" title="Hormonal contraception">hormonal contraception</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_3-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_determinants">Social determinants</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_determinants_of_obesity" title="Social determinants of obesity">Social determinants of obesity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Yamai_no_Soshi_-_Obesity.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Yamai_no_Soshi_-_Obesity.JPG/290px-Yamai_no_Soshi_-_Obesity.JPG" width="290" height="326" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/Yamai_no_Soshi_-_Obesity.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="378" data-file-height="425" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Yamai_no_Soshi_-_Obesity.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The disease scroll (Yamai no soshi, late 12th century) depicts a woman moneylender with obesity, considered a disease of the rich.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While genetic influences are important to understanding obesity, they cannot explain the current dramatic increase seen within specific countries or globally.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Though it is accepted that energy consumption in excess of energy expenditure leads to obesity on an individual basis, the cause of the shifts in these two factors on the societal scale is much debated. There are a number of theories as to the cause but most believe it is a combination of various factors.</p>
<p>The correlation between <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">social class</a> and BMI varies globally. A review in 1989 found that in developed countries women of a high social class were less likely to be obese. No significant differences were seen among men of different social classes. In the developing world, women, men, and children from high social classes had greater rates of obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> An update of this review carried out in 2007 found the same relationships, but they were weaker. The decrease in strength of correlation was felt to be due to the effects of <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-McLaren2007_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLaren2007-130">[130]</a></sup> Among developed countries, levels of adult obesity, and percentage of teenage children who are overweight, are correlated with <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">income inequality</a>. A similar relationship is seen among US states: more adults, even in higher social classes, are obese in more unequal states.<sup id="cite_ref-spirit_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spirit-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many explanations have been put forth for associations between BMI and social class. It is thought that in developed countries, the wealthy are able to afford more nutritious food, they are under greater social pressure to remain slim, and have more opportunities along with greater expectations for <a href="/wiki/Physical_fitness" title="Physical fitness">physical fitness</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Undeveloped_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Undeveloped countries">undeveloped countries</a> the ability to afford food, high energy expenditure with physical labor, and cultural values favoring a larger body size are believed to contribute to the observed patterns.<sup id="cite_ref-McLaren2007_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLaren2007-130">[130]</a></sup> Attitudes toward body weight held by people in one's life may also play a role in obesity. A correlation in BMI changes over time has been found among friends, siblings, and spouses.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Stress and perceived low social status appear to increase risk of obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-spirit_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spirit-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>Smoking has a significant effect on an individual's weight. Those who quit smoking gain an average of 4.4&#160;kilograms (9.7&#160;lb) for men and 5.0&#160;kilograms (11.0&#160;lb) for women over ten years.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> However, changing rates of smoking have had little effect on the overall rates of obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the United States the number of children a person has is related to their risk of obesity. A woman's risk increases by 7% per child, while a man's risk increases by 4% per child.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> This could be partly explained by the fact that having dependent children decreases physical activity in Western parents.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the developing world urbanization is playing a role in increasing rate of obesity. In <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> overall rates of obesity are below 5%; however, in some cities rates of obesity are greater than 20%.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">Malnutrition</a> in early life is believed to play a role in the rising rates of obesity in the <a href="/wiki/Developing_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Developing world">developing world</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DC2001_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DC2001-140">[140]</a></sup> Endocrine changes that occur during periods of malnutrition may promote the storage of fat once more food energy becomes available.<sup id="cite_ref-DC2001_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DC2001-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>Consistent with <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_epidemiology" title="Cognitive epidemiology">cognitive epidemiological</a> data, numerous studies confirm that obesity is associated with cognitive deficits.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith2011_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2011-141">[141]</a></sup> Whether obesity causes cognitive deficits, or vice versa is unclear at present.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gut_bacteria">Gut bacteria</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Infectobesity" title="Infectobesity">Infectobesity</a></div>
<p>The study of the effect of infectious agents on metabolism is still in its early stages. <a href="/wiki/Gut_flora" title="Gut flora">Gut flora</a> has been shown to differ between lean and obese humans. There is an indication that gut flora in obese and lean individuals can affect the metabolic potential. This apparent alteration of the metabolic potential is believed to confer a greater capacity to harvest energy contributing to obesity. Whether these differences are the direct cause or the result of obesity has yet to be determined unequivocally.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>An association between <a href="/wiki/Viruses" class="mw-redirect" title="Viruses">viruses</a> and obesity has been found in humans and several different animal species. The amount that these associations may have contributed to the rising rate of obesity is yet to be determined.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fatmouse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Two white mice both with similar sized ears, black eyes, and pink noses. The body of the mouse on the left, however, is about three times the width of the normal sized mouse on the right." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Fatmouse.jpg/290px-Fatmouse.jpg" width="290" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Fatmouse.jpg/435px-Fatmouse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Fatmouse.jpg/580px-Fatmouse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="435" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fatmouse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A comparison of a mouse unable to produce <a href="/wiki/Leptin" title="Leptin">leptin</a> thus resulting in obesity (left) and a normal mouse (right)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are many possible <a href="/wiki/Pathophysiology" title="Pathophysiology">pathophysiological</a> mechanisms involved in the development and maintenance of obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-flier_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flier-144">[144]</a></sup> This field of research had been almost unapproached until the <a href="/wiki/Leptin" title="Leptin">leptin</a> gene was discovered in 1994 by J. M. Friedman's laboratory.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> These investigators postulated that leptin was a satiety factor. In the ob/ob mouse, mutations in the <a href="/wiki/Leptin" title="Leptin">leptin</a> gene resulted in the obese phenotype opening the possibility of leptin therapy for human obesity. However, soon thereafter <a href="/wiki/Jose_F._Caro" title="Jose F. Caro">J. F. Caro's</a> laboratory could not detect any mutations in the leptin gene in humans with obesity. On the contrary <a href="/wiki/Leptin" title="Leptin">Leptin</a> expression was increased proposing the possibility of Leptin-resistance in human obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Since this discovery, many other hormonal mechanisms have been elucidated that participate in the regulation of <a href="/wiki/Appetite" title="Appetite">appetite</a> and food intake, storage patterns of <a href="/wiki/Adipose_tissue" title="Adipose tissue">adipose tissue</a>, and development of <a href="/wiki/Insulin_resistance" title="Insulin resistance">insulin resistance</a>. Since leptin's discovery, <a href="/wiki/Ghrelin" title="Ghrelin">ghrelin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orexin" title="Orexin">orexin</a>, <a href="/wiki/PYY_3-36" class="mw-redirect" title="PYY 3-36">PYY 3-36</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cholecystokinin" title="Cholecystokinin">cholecystokinin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adiponectin" title="Adiponectin">adiponectin</a>, as well as many other mediators have been studied. The <a href="/wiki/Adipokine" title="Adipokine">adipokines</a> are mediators produced by adipose tissue; their action is thought to modify many obesity-related diseases.</p>
<p>Leptin and ghrelin are considered to be complementary in their influence on appetite, with ghrelin produced by the stomach modulating short-term appetitive control (i.e. to eat when the stomach is empty and to stop when the stomach is stretched). Leptin is produced by adipose tissue to signal fat storage reserves in the body, and mediates long-term appetitive controls (i.e. to eat more when fat storages are low and less when fat storages are high). Although administration of leptin may be effective in a small subset of obese individuals who are leptin-deficient, most obese individuals are thought to be leptin resistant and have been found to have high levels of leptin.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> This resistance is thought to explain in part why administration of leptin has not been shown to be effective in suppressing appetite in most obese people.<sup id="cite_ref-flier_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flier-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leptin.png" class="image"><img alt="A three-dimensional model with two pairs of opposed curling columns attached together at their ends by more linear segments." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Leptin.png/290px-Leptin.png" width="290" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Leptin.png/435px-Leptin.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Leptin.png/580px-Leptin.png 2x" data-file-width="928" data-file-height="726" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leptin.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A graphic depiction of a <a href="/wiki/Leptin" title="Leptin">leptin</a> molecule</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While leptin and ghrelin are produced peripherally, they control appetite through their actions on the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a>. In particular, they and other appetite-related hormones act on the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">hypothalamus</a>, a region of the brain central to the regulation of food intake and energy expenditure. There are several circuits within the hypothalamus that contribute to its role in integrating appetite, the <a href="/wiki/Melanocortin" title="Melanocortin">melanocortin</a> pathway being the most well understood.<sup id="cite_ref-flier_144-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flier-144">[144]</a></sup> The circuit begins with an area of the hypothalamus, the <a href="/wiki/Arcuate_nucleus" title="Arcuate nucleus">arcuate nucleus</a>, that has outputs to the <a href="/wiki/Lateral_hypothalamus" title="Lateral hypothalamus">lateral hypothalamus</a> (LH) and <a href="/wiki/Ventromedial_hypothalamus" class="mw-redirect" title="Ventromedial hypothalamus">ventromedial hypothalamus</a> (VMH), the brain's feeding and satiety centers, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>The arcuate nucleus contains two distinct groups of <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-flier_144-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flier-144">[144]</a></sup> The first group coexpresses <a href="/wiki/Neuropeptide_Y" title="Neuropeptide Y">neuropeptide Y</a> (NPY) and <a href="/wiki/Agouti-related_peptide" title="Agouti-related peptide">agouti-related peptide</a> (AgRP) and has stimulatory inputs to the LH and inhibitory inputs to the VMH. The second group coexpresses <a href="/wiki/Pro-opiomelanocortin" class="mw-redirect" title="Pro-opiomelanocortin">pro-opiomelanocortin</a> (POMC) and <a href="/wiki/Cocaine-_and_amphetamine-regulated_transcript" class="mw-redirect" title="Cocaine- and amphetamine-regulated transcript">cocaine- and amphetamine-regulated transcript</a> (CART) and has stimulatory inputs to the VMH and inhibitory inputs to the LH. Consequently, NPY/AgRP neurons stimulate feeding and inhibit satiety, while POMC/CART neurons stimulate satiety and inhibit feeding. Both groups of arcuate nucleus neurons are regulated in part by leptin. Leptin inhibits the NPY/AgRP group while stimulating the POMC/CART group. Thus a deficiency in leptin signaling, either via leptin deficiency or leptin resistance, leads to overfeeding and may account for some genetic and acquired forms of obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-flier_144-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flier-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_health">Public health</span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) predicts that <a href="/wiki/Overweight" title="Overweight">overweight</a> and obesity may soon replace more traditional <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> concerns such as <a href="/wiki/Undernutrition" class="mw-redirect" title="Undernutrition">undernutrition</a> and <a href="/wiki/Infectious_diseases" class="mw-redirect" title="Infectious diseases">infectious diseases</a> as the most significant cause of poor health.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Obesity is a public health and policy problem because of its prevalence, costs, and health effects.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Preventive_Services_Task_Force" title="United States Preventive Services Task Force">United States Preventive Services Task Force</a> recommends screening for all adults followed by behavioral interventions in those who are obese.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Public health efforts seek to understand and correct the environmental factors responsible for the increasing prevalence of obesity in the population. Solutions look at changing the factors that cause excess food energy consumption and inhibit physical activity. Efforts include federally reimbursed meal programs in schools, limiting direct <a href="/wiki/Junk_food" title="Junk food">junk</a> <a href="/wiki/Food_marketing" title="Food marketing">food marketing</a> to children,<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> and decreasing access to sugar-sweetened beverages in schools.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> The World Health Organization recommends the taxing of sugary drinks.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> When constructing urban environments, efforts have been made to increase access to parks and to develop pedestrian routes.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reports">Reports</span></h3>
<p>Many organizations have published reports pertaining to obesity. In 1998, the first US Federal guidelines were published, titled "Clinical Guidelines on the Identification, Evaluation, and Treatment of Overweight and Obesity in Adults: The Evidence Report".<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> In 2006 the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Obesity_Network" title="Canadian Obesity Network">Canadian Obesity Network</a> published the "Canadian Clinical Practice Guidelines (CPG) on the Management and Prevention of Obesity in Adults and Children". This is a comprehensive evidence-based guideline to address the management and prevention of overweight and obesity in adults and children.<sup id="cite_ref-CADG2006_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CADG2006-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2004, the United Kingdom <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Physicians" title="Royal College of Physicians">Royal College of Physicians</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Faculty_of_Public_Health" title="Faculty of Public Health">Faculty of Public Health</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Paediatrics_and_Child_Health" title="Royal College of Paediatrics and Child Health">Royal College of Paediatrics and Child Health</a> released the report "Storing up Problems", which highlighted the growing problem of obesity in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> The same year, the <a href="/wiki/British_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="British House of Commons">House of Commons</a> <a href="/wiki/Health_Select_Committee" title="Health Select Committee">Health Select Committee</a> published its "most comprehensive inquiry [...] ever undertaken" into the impact of obesity on health and society in the UK and possible approaches to the problem.<sup id="cite_ref-GB2004_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GB2004-158">[158]</a></sup> In 2006, the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Health_and_Clinical_Excellence" class="mw-redirect" title="National Institute for Health and Clinical Excellence">National Institute for Health and Clinical Excellence</a> (NICE) issued a guideline on the diagnosis and management of obesity, as well as policy implications for non-healthcare organizations such as local councils.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> A 2007 report produced by <a href="/wiki/Derek_Wanless" title="Derek Wanless">Derek Wanless</a> for the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Fund" title="King's Fund">King's Fund</a> warned that unless further action was taken, obesity had the capacity to cripple the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a> financially.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>Comprehensive approaches are being looked at to address the rising rates of obesity. The Obesity Policy Action (OPA) framework divides measure into 'upstream' policies, 'midstream' policies, 'downstream' policies. 'Upstream' policies look at changing society, 'midstream' policies try to alter individuals' behavior to prevent obesity, and 'downstream' policies try to treat currently afflicted people.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Management">Management</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Management_of_obesity" title="Management of obesity">Management of obesity</a></div>
<p>The main treatment for obesity consists of <a href="/wiki/Dieting" title="Dieting">dieting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physical_exercise" title="Physical exercise">physical exercise</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CADG2006_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CADG2006-78">[78]</a></sup> Diet programs may produce <a href="/wiki/Weight_loss" title="Weight loss">weight loss</a> over the short term,<sup id="cite_ref-Strychar_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strychar-162">[162]</a></sup> but maintaining this weight loss is frequently difficult and often requires making exercise and a lower food energy diet a permanent part of a person's lifestyle.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the short-term low carbohydrate diets appear better than low fat diets for weight loss.<sup id="cite_ref-SBU2013_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SBU2013-165">[165]</a></sup> In the long term; however, all types of low-carbohydrate and low-fat diets appear equally beneficial.<sup id="cite_ref-SBU2013_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SBU2013-165">[165]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> A 2014 review found that the heart disease and diabetes risks associated with different diets appear to be similar.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Promotion of the Mediterranean diets among the obese may lower the risk of heart disease.<sup id="cite_ref-SBU2013_165-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SBU2013-165">[165]</a></sup> Decreased intake of <a href="/wiki/Sweet_drink" class="mw-redirect" title="Sweet drink">sweet drinks</a> is also related to weight-loss.<sup id="cite_ref-SBU2013_165-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SBU2013-165">[165]</a></sup> Success rates of long-term weight loss maintenance with lifestyle changes are low, ranging from 2–20%.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> Dietary and lifestyle changes are effective in limiting excessive weight gain in <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a> and improve outcomes for both the mother and the child.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> Intensive behavioral counseling is recommended in those who are both obese and have other risk factors for heart disease.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>Five medications have evidence for long-term use <a href="/wiki/Orlistat" title="Orlistat">orlistat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lorcaserin" title="Lorcaserin">lorcaserin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liraglutide" title="Liraglutide">liraglutide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phentermine/topiramate" title="Phentermine/topiramate">phentermine–topiramate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Naltrexone/bupropion" class="mw-redirect" title="Naltrexone/bupropion">naltrexone–bupropion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NEJM_2017_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NEJM_2017-171">[171]</a></sup> They result in weight loss after one year ranged from 3.0 to 6.7&#160;kg over placebo.<sup id="cite_ref-NEJM_2017_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NEJM_2017-171">[171]</a></sup> Orlistat, liraglutide, and naltrexone–bupropion are available in both the United States and Europe, whereas lorcaserin and phentermine–topiramate are available only in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-EMA2013_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EMA2013-172">[172]</a></sup> European regulatory authorities rejected the latter two drugs in part because of associations of heart valve problems with lorcaserin and more general heart and blood vessel problems with phentermine–topiramate.<sup id="cite_ref-EMA2013_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EMA2013-172">[172]</a></sup> Orlistat use is associated with high rates of gastrointestinal side effects<sup id="cite_ref-Orli07_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Orli07-173">[173]</a></sup> and concerns have been raised about negative effects on the kidneys.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> There is no information on how these drugs affect longer-term complications of obesity such as cardiovascular disease or death.<sup id="cite_ref-Yan2014_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yan2014-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most effective treatment for obesity is <a href="/wiki/Bariatric_surgery" title="Bariatric surgery">bariatric surgery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Col2014_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Col2014-9">[9]</a></sup> The types of procedures include <a href="/wiki/Laparoscopic_adjustable_gastric_banding" class="mw-redirect" title="Laparoscopic adjustable gastric banding">laparoscopic adjustable gastric banding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roux-en-Y_gastric_bypass" class="mw-redirect" title="Roux-en-Y gastric bypass">Roux-en-Y gastric bypass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vertical-sleeve_gastrectomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Vertical-sleeve gastrectomy">vertical-sleeve gastrectomy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biliopancreatic_diversion" class="mw-redirect" title="Biliopancreatic diversion">biliopancreatic diversion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NEJM_2017_171-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NEJM_2017-171">[171]</a></sup> Surgery for severe obesity is associated with long-term weight loss, improvement in obesity related conditions,<sup id="cite_ref-Chang2013_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chang2013-175">[175]</a></sup> and decreased overall mortality. One study found a weight loss of between 14% and 25% (depending on the type of procedure performed) at 10&#160;years, and a 29% reduction in all cause mortality when compared to standard weight loss measures.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> Complications occur in about 17% of cases and reoperation is needed in 7% of cases.<sup id="cite_ref-Chang2013_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chang2013-175">[175]</a></sup> Due to its cost and risks, researchers are searching for other effective yet less invasive treatments including devices that occupy space in the stomach.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Epidemiology_of_obesity" title="Epidemiology of obesity">Epidemiology of obesity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
