<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #cb0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;&gt;55%</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In earlier historical periods obesity was rare, and achievable only by a small elite, although already recognised as a problem for health. But as prosperity increased in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Modern period">Early Modern period</a>, it affected increasingly larger groups of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-Haslam2007_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haslam2007-179">[179]</a></sup> In 1997 the WHO formally recognized obesity as a global epidemic.<sup id="cite_ref-Caballero_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caballero-91">[91]</a></sup> As of 2008 the WHO estimates that at least 500&#160;million adults (greater than 10%) are obese, with higher rates among women than men.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2009a_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2009a-180">[180]</a></sup> The rate of obesity also increases with age at least up to 50 or 60&#160;years old<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> and severe obesity in the United States, Australia, and Canada is increasing faster than the overall rate of obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-morbid2007_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morbid2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pmid24351678_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24351678-182">[182]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tjepkema2005_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tjepkema2005-183">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>Once considered a problem only of high-income countries, obesity rates are rising worldwide and affecting both the developed and developing world.<sup id="cite_ref-EuroG2008_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EuroG2008-42">[42]</a></sup> These increases have been felt most dramatically in urban settings.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2009a_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2009a-180">[180]</a></sup> The only remaining region of the world where obesity is not common is <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_3-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h3>
<p><i>Obesity</i> is from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>obesitas</i>, which means "stout, fat, or plump". <i>Ēsus</i> is the past participle of <i>edere</i> (to eat), with <i>ob</i> (over) added to it.<sup id="cite_ref-etymol_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-etymol-184">[184]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Oxford_English_Dictionary" class="mw-redirect" title="The Oxford English Dictionary">The Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> documents its first usage in 1611 by <a href="/wiki/Randle_Cotgrave" title="Randle Cotgrave">Randle Cotgrave</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_attitudes">Historical attitudes</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Mellin_(attributed)_-_Portrait_of_a_Gentleman_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A very obese gentleman with a prominent double chin and mustache dressed in black with a sword at his left side." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Charles_Mellin_%28attributed%29_-_Portrait_of_a_Gentleman_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Charles_Mellin_%28attributed%29_-_Portrait_of_a_Gentleman_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="376" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Charles_Mellin_%28attributed%29_-_Portrait_of_a_Gentleman_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Charles_Mellin_%28attributed%29_-_Portrait_of_a_Gentleman_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Charles_Mellin_%28attributed%29_-_Portrait_of_a_Gentleman_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Charles_Mellin_%28attributed%29_-_Portrait_of_a_Gentleman_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="6837" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Mellin_(attributed)_-_Portrait_of_a_Gentleman_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
During the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> obesity was often seen as a sign of wealth, and was relatively common among the elite: <i>The Tuscan General <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_del_Borro" class="mw-redirect" title="Alessandro del Borro">Alessandro del Borro</a></i>, attributed to Charles Mellin, 1645<sup id="cite_ref-Zach2003_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zach2003-186">[186]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Venus_von_Willendorf_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A carved stone miniature figurine depicted an obese female." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Venus_von_Willendorf_01.jpg/220px-Venus_von_Willendorf_01.jpg" width="220" height="414" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Venus_von_Willendorf_01.jpg/330px-Venus_von_Willendorf_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Venus_von_Willendorf_01.jpg/440px-Venus_von_Willendorf_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1428" data-file-height="2684" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Venus_von_Willendorf_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Venus_of_Willendorf" title="Venus of Willendorf">Venus of Willendorf</a></i> created 24,000–22,000 BC</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_medicine" title="Ancient Greek medicine">Ancient Greek medicine</a> recognizes obesity as a medical disorder, and records that the Ancient Egyptians saw it in the same way.<sup id="cite_ref-Haslam2007_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haslam2007-179">[179]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a> wrote that "Corpulence is not only a disease itself, but the harbinger of others".<sup id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_3-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-3">[3]</a></sup> The Indian surgeon <a href="/wiki/Sushruta" title="Sushruta">Sushruta</a> (6th century BCE) related obesity to diabetes and heart disorders.<sup id="cite_ref-Dwivedi.26Dwivedi07_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dwivedi.26Dwivedi07-187">[187]</a></sup> He recommended physical work to help cure it and its side effects.<sup id="cite_ref-Dwivedi.26Dwivedi07_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dwivedi.26Dwivedi07-187">[187]</a></sup> For most of human history mankind struggled with food scarcity.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Obesity has thus historically been viewed as a sign of wealth and prosperity. It was common among high officials in Europe in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a><sup id="cite_ref-Zach2003_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zach2003-186">[186]</a></sup> as well as in Ancient East Asian civilizations.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> In the 17th century, English medical author <a href="/wiki/Tobias_Venner" title="Tobias Venner">Tobias Venner</a> is credited with being one of the first to refer to the term as a societal disease in a published English language book.<sup id="cite_ref-Haslam2007_179-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haslam2007-179">[179]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a> it was realized that the military and economic might of nations were dependent on both the body size and strength of their soldiers and workers.<sup id="cite_ref-Caballero_91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caballero-91">[91]</a></sup> Increasing the average body mass index from what is now considered underweight to what is now the normal range played a significant role in the development of industrialized societies.<sup id="cite_ref-Caballero_91-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caballero-91">[91]</a></sup> Height and weight thus both increased through the 19th&#160;century in the developed world. During the 20th century, as populations reached their genetic potential for height, weight began increasing much more than height, resulting in obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-Caballero_91-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caballero-91">[91]</a></sup> In the 1950s increasing wealth in the developed world decreased child mortality, but as body weight increased heart and kidney disease became more common.<sup id="cite_ref-Caballero_91-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caballero-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> During this time period, insurance companies realized the connection between weight and life expectancy and increased premiums for the obese.<sup id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_3-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many cultures throughout history have viewed obesity as the result of a character flaw. The <i>obesus</i> or fat character in <a href="/wiki/Greek_comedy" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek comedy">Greek comedy</a> was a glutton and figure of mockery. During Christian times the food was viewed as a gateway to the sins of <a href="/wiki/Sloth_(deadly_sin)" title="Sloth (deadly sin)">sloth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lust" title="Lust">lust</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Woodhouse_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhouse-13">[13]</a></sup> In modern Western culture, excess weight is often regarded as unattractive, and obesity is commonly associated with various negative stereotypes. People of all ages can face social stigmatization, and may be targeted by bullies or shunned by their peers.<sup id="cite_ref-Bias2001_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bias2001-192">[192]</a></sup></p>
<p>Public perceptions in Western society regarding healthy body weight differ from those regarding the weight that is considered ideal &#160;– and both have changed since the beginning of the 20th century. The weight that is viewed as an ideal has become lower since the 1920s. This is illustrated by the fact that the average height of Miss America pageant winners increased by 2% from 1922 to 1999, while their average weight decreased by 12%.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> On the other hand, people's views concerning healthy weight have changed in the opposite direction. In Britain, the weight at which people considered themselves to be overweight was significantly higher in 2007 than in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-John2008_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John2008-194">[194]</a></sup> These changes are believed to be due to increasing rates of adiposity leading to increased acceptance of extra body fat as being normal.<sup id="cite_ref-John2008_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John2008-194">[194]</a></sup></p>
<p>Obesity is still seen as a sign of wealth and well-being in many parts of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>. This has become particularly common since the <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> epidemic began.<sup id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_3-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_arts">The arts</span></h3>
<p>The first sculptural representations of the human body 20,000–35,000&#160;years ago depict obese females. Some attribute the <a href="/wiki/Venus_figurines" title="Venus figurines">Venus figurines</a> to the tendency to emphasize fertility while others feel they represent "fatness" in the people of the time.<sup id="cite_ref-Woodhouse_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhouse-13">[13]</a></sup> Corpulence is, however, absent in both Greek and Roman art, probably in keeping with their ideals regarding moderation. This continued through much of Christian European history, with only those of low socioeconomic status being depicted as obese.<sup id="cite_ref-Woodhouse_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhouse-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> some of the upper class began flaunting their large size, as can be seen in portraits of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII of England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_del_Borro" class="mw-redirect" title="Alessandro del Borro">Alessandro del Borro</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Woodhouse_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhouse-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Paul_Rubens" title="Peter Paul Rubens">Rubens</a> (1577–1640) regularly depicted full-bodied women in his pictures, from which derives the term <a href="/wiki/Rubenesque" class="mw-redirect" title="Rubenesque">Rubenesque</a>. These women, however, still maintained the "hourglass" shape with its relationship to fertility.<sup id="cite_ref-Fumento_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fumento-195">[195]</a></sup> During the 19th&#160;century, views on obesity changed in the Western world. After centuries of obesity being synonymous with wealth and social status, slimness began to be seen as the desirable standard.<sup id="cite_ref-Woodhouse_13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhouse-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_impact">Economic impact</span></h3>
<p>In addition to its health impacts, obesity leads to many problems including disadvantages in employment<sup id="cite_ref-Puhl_R._p.29_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Puhl_R._p.29-196">[196]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> and increased business costs. These effects are felt by all levels of society from individuals, to corporations, to governments.</p>
<p>In 2005, the medical costs attributable to obesity in the US were an estimated $190.2&#160;billion or 20.6% of all medical expenditures,<sup id="cite_ref-medical-costs_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-medical-costs-198">[198]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> while the cost of obesity in Canada was estimated at CA$2 billion in 1997 (2.4% of total health costs).<sup id="cite_ref-CADG2006_78-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CADG2006-78">[78]</a></sup> The total annual direct cost of overweight and obesity in Australia in 2005 was A$21 billion. Overweight and obese Australians also received A$35.6 billion in government subsidies.<sup id="cite_ref-MJA2009_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MJA2009-201">[201]</a></sup> The estimate range for annual expenditures on diet products is $40&#160;billion to $100&#160;billion in the US alone.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup></p>
<p>Obesity prevention programs have been found to reduce the cost of treating obesity-related disease. However, the longer people live, the more medical costs they incur. Researchers, therefore, conclude that reducing obesity may improve the public's health, but it is unlikely to reduce overall health spending.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wide_Chair.jpg" class="image"><img alt="An extra wide chair beside a number of normal sized chairs." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Wide_Chair.jpg/290px-Wide_Chair.jpg" width="290" height="244" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Wide_Chair.jpg/435px-Wide_Chair.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c4/Wide_Chair.jpg 2x" data-file-width="543" data-file-height="457" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wide_Chair.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Services must accommodate obese people with specialist equipment such as much wider chairs.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Obesity can lead to social stigmatization and disadvantages in employment.<sup id="cite_ref-Puhl_R._p.29_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Puhl_R._p.29-196">[196]</a></sup> When compared to their normal weight counterparts, obese workers on average have higher rates of absenteeism from work and take more disability leave, thus increasing costs for employers and decreasing productivity.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> A study examining Duke University employees found that people with a BMI over 40&#160;kg/m<sup>2</sup> filed twice as many <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_compensation" title="Workers' compensation">workers' compensation</a> claims as those whose BMI was 18.5–24.9&#160;kg/m<sup>2</sup>. They also had more than 12&#160;times as many lost work days. The most common injuries in this group were due to falls and lifting, thus affecting the lower extremities, wrists or hands, and backs.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> The Alabama State Employees' Insurance Board approved a controversial plan to charge obese workers $25 a month for health insurance that would otherwise be free unless they take steps to lose weight and improve their health. These measures started in January 2010 and apply to those state workers whose BMI exceeds 35&#160;kg/m<sup>2</sup> and who fail to make improvements in their health after one year.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some research shows that obese people are less likely to be hired for a job and are less likely to be promoted.<sup id="cite_ref-Bias2001_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bias2001-192">[192]</a></sup> Obese people are also paid less than their non-obese counterparts for an equivalent job; obese women on average make 6% less and obese men make 3% less.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup></p>
<p>Specific industries, such as the airline, healthcare and food industries, have special concerns. Due to rising rates of obesity, airlines face higher fuel costs and pressures to increase seating width.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> In 2000, the extra weight of obese passengers cost airlines US$275&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> The healthcare industry has had to invest in special facilities for handling severely obese patients, including special lifting equipment and <a href="/wiki/Bariatric_ambulance" title="Bariatric ambulance">bariatric ambulances</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> Costs for restaurants are increased by litigation accusing them of causing obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-Govtrack_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Govtrack-212">[212]</a></sup> In 2005 the US Congress discussed legislation to prevent civil lawsuits against the food industry in relation to obesity; however, it did not become law.<sup id="cite_ref-Govtrack_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Govtrack-212">[212]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the <a href="/wiki/American_Medical_Association" title="American Medical Association">American Medical Association</a>'s 2013 classification of obesity as a chronic disease,<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes20130618_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes20130618-14">[14]</a></sup> it is thought that health insurance companies will more likely pay for obesity treatment, counseling and surgery, and the cost of research and development of fat treatment pills or gene therapy treatments should be more affordable if insurers help to subsidize their cost.<sup id="cite_ref-WashPost20130620_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WashPost20130620-213">[213]</a></sup> The AMA classification is not legally binding, however, so health insurers still have the right to reject coverage for a treatment or procedure.<sup id="cite_ref-WashPost20130620_213-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WashPost20130620-213">[213]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2014, The European Court of Justice ruled that morbid obesity is a disability. The Court argued that if an employee's obesity prevents him from "full and effective participation of that person in professional life on an equal basis with other workers", then it shall be considered a disability and that firing someone on such grounds is discriminatory.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size_acceptance">Size acceptance</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PresidentTaftTelephoneCrop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/PresidentTaftTelephoneCrop.jpg/290px-PresidentTaftTelephoneCrop.jpg" width="290" height="467" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/PresidentTaftTelephoneCrop.jpg/435px-PresidentTaftTelephoneCrop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/PresidentTaftTelephoneCrop.jpg/580px-PresidentTaftTelephoneCrop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="694" data-file-height="1117" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PresidentTaftTelephoneCrop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/United_States_President" class="mw-redirect" title="United States President">United States President</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a> was often ridiculed for being overweight</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fat_acceptance_movement" title="Fat acceptance movement">Fat acceptance movement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_stigma_of_obesity" title="Social stigma of obesity">Social stigma of obesity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Health_at_Every_Size" title="Health at Every Size">Health at Every Size</a></div>
<p>The principal goal of the fat acceptance movement is to decrease discrimination against people who are overweight and obese.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> However, some in the movement are also attempting to challenge the established relationship between obesity and negative health outcomes.<sup id="cite_ref-Pulver2007_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pulver2007-217">[217]</a></sup></p>
<p>A number of organizations exist that promote the acceptance of obesity. They have increased in prominence in the latter half of the 20th&#160;century.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> The US-based <a href="/wiki/National_Association_to_Advance_Fat_Acceptance" title="National Association to Advance Fat Acceptance">National Association to Advance Fat Acceptance</a> (NAAFA) was formed in 1969 and describes itself as a civil rights organization dedicated to ending size discrimination.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Size_Acceptance_Association" title="International Size Acceptance Association">International Size Acceptance Association</a> (ISAA) is a <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">non-governmental organization</a> (NGO) which was founded in 1997. It has more of a global orientation and describes its mission as promoting size acceptance and helping to end weight-based discrimination.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> These groups often argue for the recognition of obesity as a disability under the US <a href="/wiki/Americans_With_Disabilities_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Americans With Disabilities Act">Americans With Disabilities Act</a> (ADA). The American legal system, however, has decided that the potential public health costs exceed the benefits of extending this anti-discrimination law to cover obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-Pulver2007_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pulver2007-217">[217]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry_influence_on_research">Industry influence on research</span></h3>
<p>In 2015 the <i>New York Times</i> published an article on the <a href="/wiki/Global_Energy_Balance_Network" title="Global Energy Balance Network">Global Energy Balance Network</a>, a nonprofit founded in 2014 that advocated for people to focus on increasing exercise rather than reducing calorie intake to avoid obesity and to be healthy. The organization was founded with at least $1.5M in funding from the <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Coca-Cola Company">Coca-Cola Company</a> and the company had provided $4M in research funding to the two founding scientists Gregory A. Hand and Steven N. Blair, since 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NesleJAMA_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NesleJAMA-222">[222]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_obesity">Childhood obesity</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Childhood_obesity" title="Childhood obesity">Childhood obesity</a></div>
<p>The healthy BMI range varies with the age and sex of the child. Obesity in children and adolescents is defined as a BMI greater than the 95th&#160;<a href="/wiki/Percentile" title="Percentile">percentile</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cdc.gov_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cdc.gov-21">[21]</a></sup> The reference data that these percentiles are based on is from 1963 to 1994 and thus has not been affected by the recent increases in rates of obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-Flegal_KM.2C_Ogden_CL.2C_Wei_R.2C_Kuczmarski_RL.2C_Johnson_CL_2001_1086.E2.80.9393_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flegal_KM.2C_Ogden_CL.2C_Wei_R.2C_Kuczmarski_RL.2C_Johnson_CL_2001_1086.E2.80.9393-22">[22]</a></sup> Childhood obesity has reached epidemic proportions in the 21st&#160;century, with rising rates in both the developed and the developing world. Rates of obesity in Canadian boys have increased from 11% in the 1980s to over 30% in the 1990s, while during this same time period rates increased from 4 to 14% in Brazilian children.<sup id="cite_ref-flynn2006_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flynn2006-223">[223]</a></sup></p>
<p>As with obesity in adults, many factors contribute to the rising rates of childhood obesity. Changing diet and decreasing physical activity are believed to be the two most important causes for the recent increase in the incidence of child obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> Because childhood obesity often persists into adulthood and is associated with numerous chronic illnesses, children who are obese are often tested for <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">hypertension</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" class="mw-redirect" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyperlipidemia" title="Hyperlipidemia">hyperlipidemia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fatty_liver" title="Fatty liver">fatty liver</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CADG2006_78-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CADG2006-78">[78]</a></sup> Treatments used in children are primarily lifestyle interventions and behavioral techniques, although efforts to increase activity in children have had little success.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid23044984_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23044984-225">[225]</a></sup> In the United States, medications are not FDA approved for use in this age group.<sup id="cite_ref-flynn2006_223-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flynn2006-223">[223]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">Other animals</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Obesity_in_pets" title="Obesity in pets">Obesity in pets</a></div>
<p>Obesity in pets is common in many countries. In the United States, 23–41% of dogs are overweight, and about 5.1% are obese.<sup id="cite_ref-Lund2006_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lund2006-226">[226]</a></sup> The rate of obesity in cats was slightly higher at 6.4%.<sup id="cite_ref-Lund2006_226-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lund2006-226">[226]</a></sup> In Australia the rate of obesity among dogs in a veterinary setting has been found to be 7.6%.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> The risk of obesity in dogs is related to whether or not their owners are obese; however, there is no similar correlation between cats and their owners.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
